Chapter XXXII.
On the Cure of Teeth Elevated above Others.
When the teeth are elevated and not according to their natural form, and especially when they are in women, and even more so in venerable old ladies; therefore it is necessary that you consider: if a tooth is too close to another tooth, and there is no possible suture or ligament of it, then carefully remove it; but if it is not attached to another tooth, then you will incise it with this instrument whose form is as follows; and it resembles the jawbone of a pig, and is made of Indian steel with an extremely sharp extension.
And be your incision in which you will incise that tooth for many days due to the hardness of the tooth, and so that it is not confused with another tooth.
And if the elevated tooth is exposed to decay, then carefully file it with a file of Indian steel whose form is this:
And be it entirely of Indian steel and its margin finely sharpened; and be it with many small points just like a file in which needles are made, on which the tooth is filed gradually over many days with ease, so that another tooth is not confused and clogged. And then carefully file it at the end, and remove it with some rasors.
And if it is a tooth of which one part is broken and the tongue lies over it, then carefully file that tooth until the sharpness of that fracture is removed and equalized, and be filled in order that the tongue may not lie over it or corrupt the word.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the Restoration of Damaged Teeth with Gold or Silver Filling.
When accidents occur to the teeth before concussion and removal by percussion or by chance, and the patient cannot withstand what is happening, and you have cured that tooth with stiptic medicines, and it has not been effective, then carefully prepare in those that are strong with a gold filling and not silver, for silver turns green and soon becomes weak after some days, but gold is never changed.
And be the filling in such subtlety and thickness according to the amount that can enter between the teeth; and the form of the filling is that you take the filling and take double its length between two healthy teeth, and carefully increase it with two bends of the filling between two damaged ones, or if one or many, until the filling reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And then carefully return to the original position, and strengthen your hand with ease and wisdom until it is not damaged.
And be your grip such that you strengthen the filling so that it does not escape from the roots of the teeth.
And then carefully incise the superfluous edges of the filling with forceps or tweezers, and add them to each place with gestures, and convert them and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged one, so that they do not cause injury to the tongue. And then carefully let it set just like a stone.
But if it is loosened or broken, strengthen that one or those with another filling in such a manner for all its time.
And this is the form of teeth and the figure of the restoration of two healthy teeth and two damaged ones, just like these linked:
And when it is done that one tooth or two returns to its place, and are re-grown just like we have recounted, and remain.
And only a good craftsman and wise person can do this.
And sometimes it is suitable for some reason of cow bones, and it is made from them just like the shape of a tooth, and is placed in a location where the tooth falls out, and is strengthened as we have said, and remains and serves that one for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the Incision of the Ligament which is in the Tongue and Prohibits Speech.
This ligament which is sometimes found behind the tongue or is natural to it and one has not noticed it before, or accidental and occurs by accident of a wound which has already been consolidated.
And the operation on it is that you open the mouth of the patient, and his existing tongue is in your sight; and lift up his tongue, and then carefully incise that ligament nerve according to its latitude, until the tongue is freed from its recent constriction.
But if in that ligament there is some hardness or knotting, and it has been set by the consolidation of the wound, then you will take a thread and carefully cut it through its latitude until you have divided the ligament and the knotting is dissolved.
And beware that there is not a false fissure in the prepuce of the flesh and that you cut here artery and bring forth flow of blood.
And then carefully wash the wound of the incision with water, vinegar, or fregged water; and then pause beside the tongue to relax it; thus is the way which keeps the patient's tongue relaxed in each night so that it will not consolidate again.
But if there is a flow of blood, pause over the place with vitriol powder; but if the blood flows, then carefully cauterize the place with caustic lanolin which is suitable for this. And then carefully cure it with other causes until it is healed.
Chapter XXXV.
On the Extraction of a Ranula behind the Tongue.
Sometimes, behind the tongue, an abscess forms similar to a small boil that prohibits the tongue from performing its natural function; and very soon it is magnified until it fills the mouth.
And the operation on it is that you open the patient's mouth so that he is sitting in front of the sun and carefully observe the abscess.
And if you see it of a fiery red or black, hard, and the patient does not feel any sensation, then do not touch it because it is cancerous.
And if it is softened to whiteness, in which there is moisture, then you put the onion into it, and carefully incise that with a fine spatula, and cut it off from all sides; and if blood comes out at the time of your operation, then you pause above a powder scattered, that is vitriol, until the bleeding is stopped.
And then return to your work until you have completed it in accordance with the place, until it is washed with vinegar and salt; and cure that with another cure suitable for this, until it is healed.
Chapter XXXVI.
On the Extraction of an Abscess from Both Ears and that which is nothing in the Gullet and other Abscesses.
When one endures in the Gullet a swelling, which are glands that are assembled together, which accidesc to the outside and are called two Ears, which when you cure them with what we have said in the division and it is not healed, then you consider if the abscess is hard, of fiery red color, and has little sensation, then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of a red color and the root is thick, then do not touch that with iron for the flow of blood; before leave that to mature, and then either you will extract it or break it apart by itself.
And if it is of a white color, rough, and the root of it is subtle, then that which is needed is to cut it.
And the operation on it is that you consider, before your operation, if the abscess has already repaid its debt with complete rest or if it has decreased by some diminution; and then make the patient sit in front of the sun, and his head is in your sight, and open his mouth, and the servant takes hold of his tongue behind with an instrument of noble form:
And be made of silver or iron, and be subtle like a knife.
Then when you take hold of that tongue and the abscess is shown to you, and you gaze upon it with your own sight, then take an onion and place it on top of that, and pull it out towards the outside parts as much as you can. Or another way is to take some cause of tunics, and then carefully incise that with an instrument whose form is this:
Or another way is to take some cause of tunics, and then carefully incise that with an instrument whose form is this: and it is assembled with forceps, however the edges of it are curved, so that the orifice of each one is in the end of the other, very sharp, which is made of Indian steel well tempered.
But if this instrument is not to your hand, then carefully incise that with a spatula whose form is this: And be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have incised one notch, incise the other according to this same manner of incision, and then carefully give the patient a drink of water with vinegar.
But if there is a flow of blood, give the patient a drink of decoction of scorsone of myrtle or similar to that of other stiptic causes until the bleeding is stopped, and then carefully cure that one until it is healed.
And sometimes there are other abscesses in the gullet besides those two ears, and incise them according to what I have said in the incision of two notches equally.
And I had already cured a woman of an abscess which was located in her gullet, which abscess tended to become fiery red, with little sensation, and had already closed off the gullet, and the woman was breathing very tightly, and had already forbidden herself from eating or drinking water, and had almost reached death if I had not acted for a day or two.
And the abscess had so swollen that it had formed two ridges and was about to burst through two holes.
Therefore I carefully cut open the fistula and remained in one of them, and pulled out that abscess, and it was drawn out with a great deal of discharge, and then I incised the other one from where it comprehended the other hole, and then I opened her mouth, and grasped her tongue, and then I placed the onion into that abscess.
And then I carefully cut out a great part of it, and there was only a little bleeding. Therefore the gullet of that woman was absolutely free, and she began to drink water soon after, and then I carefully prescribed food for her. And so I did not cease to incise that abscess many times over a long period, and successive abscesses appeared in its place, which I incised until it was prolonged.
Therefore I went and cauterized the gullet of her, therefore above addition; and then I walked away and did not know what God had done for me.
Chapter XXXVII.
On the Incision of an Umbilical Abscess when it is Apostemated.
When the umbilical cord is diseased in the navel, and it is apostemated and is long, then it is called a "perpendicular" one; and if it is on the side that is thick and rounded, then it is called a "bubble".
But when it is cured with what we have said in the division, and your cure does not agree with it, and it happens that the abscess has already gone away from there and is subtle, then you must incise that which is added on roundly; and if it reaches that length, and is sanguine, fiery red or black, and has no sensation in it, then you must fear the incision of it, because in that place there is fear for the patient.
And when you incise that according to the manner which I have said of its whiteness and length, then you make the patient sit and take hold of his tongue with an instrument whose form is this: And carefully place the onion in the navel, and pull it down and incise it with one of two instruments which are mentioned in the incision of two notches.
And it is necessary that you do not incise that, but only as much as is adjusted to the natural term without addition, because if you incise too much, you will deprive him of his speech.
And then carefully incise it with that which we have mentioned in the incision of two notches, and cure that one until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision of it, then you must use great care in the cauterization of it without fear, and the manner of the cauterization is nothing but with sharp water, and that you pause the head of the patient in your sight, and carefully grasp his tongue with the instrument which we have mentioned, and take from the water which we have said in the chapter on cauterization, and confine it to that living cause, and pause that no thick or subtle, and fill that concavity with the instrument whose form is this:
And let the edge of it be in the concavity in which the medicine is paused, just like the concavity of a spoon, and pause the instrument with the medicine on the navel, and the patient will stretch out his hand over his own side so that he may pour saliva or mouthwash from his mouth into that concavity through the intrinsic medicine, so that none of those causes will descend onto the gullet of him and trouble it.
And then carefully take your hand and the medicine, and express that onto the uvula for a space of half an hour until you see it to be discolored and sedated by the biting of the medicine.
And if you wish, take a cloth and wrap it around the extremity of the ray, and immerse the cloth in the medicine, and insert the ray with the cloth into a syringe or needle, and make sure that the cloth adheres to the syringe or needle, and do this as many times as you need until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, and then let it be so that it dries out and falls off about three days or four.
And if you need to iterate the medicine, iterate it, and then carefully cauterize it in the circuit of the uvula with cloth wrapped around it, and clean off any residue from the circuit of the medicine, and wash it thoroughly with vinegar water, and treat it externally with embrocation, and internally with gargling, until it is healed.
And this kind of cure is even more saved than incision and less long in fear.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with something even more subtle than cauterization and incision, because it is treated by subfumigation in this manner: take calament, myrrh, origanum, ruda, Arabic scicados, camomile, broth, and similar herbs to those of plants, and mix all those causes, or some of them, into a bath, and let them be submerged in vinegar water, and boil; and the bath should be well stirred with a decent stirring, and there should be a hole in the middle of the bath, on which should be placed an instrument made of iron or steel according to this manner.
And the extremity should be inserted into the mouth of the patient so that the vapor rises to the uvula on the quinsy, until the uvula is quite obscured.
And then repeat this process many times until it is dry, and be careful not to apply this cure at the beginning of the appearance of abscesses, because it would be too intense for the abscess, and you should not do that, but rather declare the appearance of the abscess first.
But if you do not have this instrument, then take a reed and compose in the extremity of it a bundle of cotton, so that the mouth of the patient may be closed, because the closure of the cotton prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this cure is good for health.
Chapter XXXVIII.
On the Extraction of Thorns and of what adheres in the Gullet and of other Causes.
Many times something adheres in the gullet or thorn from a fish or some other cause.
Then you must extract what is apparent from those things that are seen above the gullet, because you grasp the tongue with an instrument in front of the sun, so that it may be declared to you what is in the throat.
And what does not appear and is hidden to you in the gullet, then you must make the patient vomit before the food has been digested in the stomach, because the attached cause to the gullet will be forgotten by vomiting; or that the patient drinks a potion of nape or of razit of laytuga or a bowl of dry bread; or takes a piece of marine sponge, and binds it tightly with a thread, and then makes the patient swallow it, so that when it reaches the place where the thorns are and is extracted, then they will be forgotten. And then take a snake and bind it tightly with a thread, and place it inside the patient's mouth and then pull it out; or take a fork and place it inside the patient's mouth and then pull it out.
But if those causes are not forgotten as we have said, then take an instrument of lead according to this form:
And be more thick than a reed, a little one, and in the extremity of it be a concavity which you insert into the patient's gullet with ease.
And it will make the patient's head rise upwards, and be careful that the touch of its bone in the throat is gentle, so that it does not disturb the flesh or the spine, or put pressure on the bone or the thorn.
But meanwhile, the patient will move that part of his body into a sweet position, by virtue of his knowledge of the location of the attached cause and its descent below, and pull his hand with the instrument towards him, all according to the quantity which is prepared in advance until it is forgotten.
Chapter XXXIX.
On the Extraction of Blood from the Gullet.
When blood is medicated with what we have said in the division of cure with medicines and does not agree, then consider that in the gullet of the patient to the sun, because you have grasped his tongue with an instrument which we have recommended.
Therefore, if you see blood, extract it with a small onion or with subtle gestures decently.
And if it is not possible to extract it with that, then take a concave syringe and insert it into the patient's gullet, and carefully pause in the concavity of the syringe an iron hot instrument, and do this many times (until the blood is dead and comes out of the gullet) and abstain from giving the patient water all day.
And then take a vial full of vinegar water, and work the patient's mouth with that, and wash the mouth with that; and do not let any cause emerge from that and spoil the water for an hour afterwards by putting it in the patient's hand, because the blood will soon come out when it feels the water.
But if it is not forgotten as we have recommended, then subfumigate the patient with camphor or with assafetida by the instrument which we have recommended in the subfumigation of the uvula.
This is how it will be, and then it will fall out.
And there is another manner of operation in the subfumigation of that, which is: you take a bath in which are hot coals and the bath is covered with a cloth in the middle of which is a hole; and the extremity of the instrument is composed in that hole; and then carefully wait above the subfumigation; and have the patient's mouth open at the extremity of the syringe, and close their mouth so that the subfumigation is not forgotten until you know that the subfumigation has reached the blood, for the blood will soon fall out.
And if it does not fall out, then you must iterate the subfumigation many times, and abstain from giving the patient water to drink, and give them salty causes and salted foods, and not give them vinegar water, and so it is necessary that with this regimen it will be forgotten.
And this is the form of the instrument with which blood is drawn from the gullet when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps like those, but with some curvature. The one that is placed in the gullet, and its extremity is like a stork's beak, which is grasped, in which a lime is sharpened, so that when it compresses any cause, that one does not loosen.
Chapter XL.
In which is the summary of the sermon in warning against abscesses and their section.
Abscesses are many species, of many manners, according to the memory of them, each one in this book.
And thus diverse in perforation and section of them by two manners, of which one is by the species of abscesses themselves; and therefore that they comprehend the humors.
And the second manner is according to the places in which they occur in the body, because an abscess that occurs on the head is another from one that occurs on the tailbone; and an abscess in a fatty location is another from one that occurs at a joint; and each of them has its own judgment by operation.
And besides, some abscesses are such that they must not be pierced, but rather allowed to mature and heal on their own.
And some abscesses are such that they must be pierced when they are crossed, and not allowed to mature according to their natural course, just like those abscesses that are near joints, for an abscess that occurs near a joint is prolonged into the circuit of the joint, and then it is corrupted by affecting the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing contraction of that member; or it is an abscess near the main limb, and if you delay the puncture until the abscess has matured on the main limb, or it is an abscess near the anus, so pierce it when not yet mature; and do not pierce a green abscess, without proper digestion, but only that which is near the anus, in order to avoid prolonging the passage of stool and causing a fistula, or that it becomes a condition that cannot be cured. But you must know the time of puncturing the abscess, and thus is when it has matured.
And you must know that it is mature when there is cessation of pain and fever, and accordingly the decrease in swelling and pulse, and the sharpness of the tip of the abscess, and other signs.
And thus it is necessary to know that the puncture should be made in the most dependent part of the abscess and closest to the cure of the prominence.
And puncture should be made through the length of flesh or muscle if the abscesses are in the parts of the arms or legs, and in areas of the loins, cords of nerves, or tendons; and finally, in all locations where there is no reflection; because in those that are reflected, use puncture according to that member.
And when the abscess is in a fatty location, then it is better that you wait until the perforation of it is confirmed by the digestion of it according to its natural course, as we have said; because if you puncture it too soon, it will prolong the process of the body's discharge and much pus, and therefore the lips of the abscess will be hardened and its depth increased.
But some abscesses are punctured according to the necessity of the location of the flesh or muscle, or according to what the affected member demands.
And it is necessary to administer a small puncture, and in large abscesses, a large puncture or multiple sections according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And some abscesses are such that the cord needs to be concaved and incised, when it is done in a way that is already stated, like those that occur in troops of boils and buboes.
And some abscesses are such that they are incised with a section having three angles; and of those is the one which is incised like the figure of a myrtle leaf, as with those abscesses of the gout sufferers.
And some abscesses are such that they receive a circular section and a lunar section, and resemble those.
But as for the one that is not like those abscesses of lions, which are superficial and do not have a deep or complex shape, it is necessary to pierce it with a superficial puncture or only a flat one.
And it is necessary when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, that you pierce it and do not hesitate to extract all the pus in that hour; having extracted one part, and then stop the abscess until the next day, and then extract a larger part, and so on many times according to gradation until it is forgotten.
And especially if the patient is a young child, or an elderly person in decline, because the animal spirit many times is resolved through the egression of pus, and then the patient will die, and you should intervene.
Therefore, keep this chapter with great caution, and carefully perform the puncture in which you pierce these abscesses, so that you cleanse the plagues and consider whether the plague of the abscess is small, or is a flat section; then administer those of like or similar things; and if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then it is necessary to pause in each track until they are together.
And if in the abscess one part of the cord has been severed or has been concaved in the circuit, then it is necessary to complete it with old cloth or a thread made from linen fabric, just as when you are binding pans or razors, its moisture; and strengthen that within three days; and accordingly process it and cure the patient with the same method until they are healed.
But if bleeding occurs during your operation, then you should administer vinegar water and wine mixed with linen cloth, and pause that over the location of the bleeding many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then it is necessary to administer a regimen and cure, and dust those which I have recommended in many places of this our book and division.
But if it is in the time of winter, and the location of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then it is necessary to wrap powders in vinegar and oil, warm them, and pause them over those locations.
And if it is in summer, and the locations are fleshy, then it is necessary to pause powders wrapped in water and oil or vinegar and oil, and all should be cool within three days, just as we have said, and then you must loosen the abscess and cleanse it, and administer the cure according to what is suitable for it until it is healed.
And thus this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the cure of abscesses universally, but according to the way of distinction that I have said about each abscess in which manner is the way of its cure by chapters separately.
Chapter XLI.
On the Section of Abscesses that occur in the Cord of the Head.
In the cord of the head small abscesses occur, and there are species of nodes that contain tunics which are according to their facts; and the species of them are many.
And among them are pustules, and those that contain moisture similar to lime. And among them are those that contain moisture similar to powder, and resembling their own kind; and those that are lithoid, hard.
And in the section of all, and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, as you should not make the extraction cause contraction of the artery.
And the operation in section of those is that you express them with an instrument whose form is similar to this, which is called an entrume, until you know what it contains.
Therefore, if what is contained is moisture, then cut it with a section according to its length, in a superficial section following this form:
And begin in the section of the line .B. up to the line .G. Then, as the moisture is evacuated, cut off that portion which contains that moisture, and do not leave any cause behind, for many times it returns, if some cause remains.
And accordingly, submerge the cloth in Egyptian ointment, if present, and if not, in water and salt; and fill it with that and leave it for another day, for by this, you will corrode all that remains of that skin.
And accordingly, iterate over that cloth with Egyptian ointment secondly and thirdly if it is needed of it, until you are confident that there is no remaining cause of that skin; then cure the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodes, then cut into it according to this form: with a section having the form of a cross, and cut into it between two lips of the section and scrape off all that part, and strain your virtue in extracting the tunic which contains that.
But if your artery becomes contracted, then do what I have recounted to you first.
And similarly do with the lithoid abscess of section and cure according to what we have said; and the section over the lithoid abscess is more gentle, because it has little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes there are cases of these abscesses which are in the head of some men who do not have moisture; and this cause is because you incise over an abscess on the head of an old woman and break open the abscess like a deaf, harsh, white palsy that could not be shaken off, and if any force was applied to it, it would become infected.
But in other abscesses that occur on the head, it is not this chapter, such as those that occur on the heads of children and in the crevices of the ears, but the section of all these shall be superficial.
And pause the puncture at the lower part of them, so that it may be alleviated the body from the pus to the lowest parts.
And accordingly, cure those with what is suitable for its cure.
Chapter XLII.
On the Section of Scrofulas which occur in the Neck.
Many times these abscesses occur in the neck and armpits and shoulders; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.
And those that occur in the neck, and many which are generated between them, and all the scrofula of those is surrounded by its own tunic, just as with the nodes and abscesses of the head, just as we have recounted.
And the species of these scrofulas are many, for some are lithoid, and some contain moisture, and some are malignant which do not yield to cure.
Therefore, in that one which you find to be of good disposition in the Tocament, whose manifest color is akin to the color of the cord, and is spread out over all parts, and not annexed to the nerve of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, and does not swell, then you should incise it with a superficial section from above to below the body along the line of .G. up to the line of .B., and scrape off that part completely, and extend two lips of the cord with one or many oncements if necessary, just as we have said in the abscesses of the head, and extract it little by little.
And be more cautious so that the vein or nerve is not cut.
And be careful not to cut too deep, lest you adjust your hand to your own mark or wound the patient, or else you would inscribe something that is not necessary to inscribe; for if you cut into a vein or artery and prohibit your operation, then pause in the wound, whether it is a bruise or other powder that stops bleeding, and strengthen the wound, and leave it until the sharpness of the abscess has subsided and the wound has become softened and begins to heal. When the blood is severed.
Therefore, return to your operation until you have completed it.
And accordingly, inquire about the sign given by your teacher if there is any remaining cause of other small scrofulas, then incise those and clean them.
And if it is in the root of the large scrofula, then it is necessary that you do not incise that scrofula from its roots. Therefore, bind it with a double thread and leave it until it falls off on its own without causing harm.
And accordingly, fill the wound with old cloth which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment, and accordingly cure that.
And if you incise that scrofula completely, then it is necessary that you adjust the two lips of the wound and close them as soon as possible so that there remains no superfluous excess.
But if you find that there remains excess due to the greatness of the scrofula, then it is necessary that you understand how to incise its root, and extract it, and administer suture and what is said.
And that of the scrofulas which contain moisture, pierce it with a superficial puncture at the location where you find the point of maturation of it, and pause puncture at the lowest part of the body, just as we have said.
And accordingly, administer puncture with ointment and similar substances to those, in order that it may corrode what remains of the corruption. And accordingly, when you find that the wound is already cleansed, then cure it with ointment of generated flesh until it is healed.
Chapter XLIII.
On the Section of Pilling off the Abscess which occurs in the Groin.
The ancients have described this section in the pillography, and I have not seen any man in our land who has practiced it.
And this is the inscription of the sermon of those.
But in men with hemorrhoids, it is necessary to avoid the extraction of the pillography, because through this, there would be no advantage for the patient, since all the veins and main vessels are here.
But in those who have abscesses located in the mouth or groin or both ears, when the disease is not in the cane, it is necessary to use the section of pillography to flee from the destruction which is caused by perforation.
Therefore, when we want to do this, we must incise the pillography behind three circles of the pillography, or four with a small section along the latitude in that which is between two circles by quantity, so that the section be in ciphac, not in cartilage, and this location is suitable for section, because it is deprived of flesh and the vessels of blood are nearby.
And if the doctor is fearful, then it is necessary that he extends the cord of the groin with one incision, and accordingly cuts the cord into the channel, but avoids the vessels of blood if he sees any cause.
And accordingly, incise the ciphac according to what we have recounted; and it is signified about the section of the channel by the flame which is from that place and comes forth with water which is broken down and by the absorption of the voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Therefore, as the hour is re-matured in which puncture is feared, add two lips of the cord of the wound, and close it alone except for the cartilage.
And accordingly, administer medicines that make the flesh to heal, until it is healed.
And Albucasis says that the summary of this sermon which we have recounted is nothing but to see that some of these abscesses have opened up the groin of the patient, and the patient has come to death, and the pulse of life is interrupted, and some are about to incise the epigastrium so that the patient may breathe over the location of the wound by some respiration, and be saved from death.
And therefore, I command to leave the wound open until the evil of the disease is consumed; and this evil lasts for three days; and therefore, I command that way the wound, and cure it until it is healed.
And what I have seen with my own eyes is that a servant took a cote and placed it in his groin and incised a part of the canal of the lung, and you were called to its cure, and opened up the mouth as if it was crying out like one who is dying, and discovered the wound and opened up the blood which was flowing from the small part of the wound.
Therefore, you were confessed that it had not cut into a vein nor a nerve, and the wind was coming from the wound, and cured it until it was healed.
And there was no harm to the servant except that he grew hoarse in his voice, and accordingly some days returned to better disposition.
And now I say to you that in the section of pillography, there is no fear.
Chapter XLIV.
On the Section of Abscess which occurs in the Groin outside and is called Hernia of the Groin.
This abscess is named Hernia of the Groin and is a large abscess according to the color of the body, and it is more common in women.
And it is according to two manners, either natural or accidental.
And in the natural one, there is no way to cure it.
And the accidental one is according to two manners of which one is similar to fatty nodes, and the other manner is similar to abscesses that are caused by the constriction of an artery, and in that one there is fear. And it is not necessary to make assumptions about it unless it is small. And you should proceed with that and investigate it according to the dictum, and if you find it similar to fatty nodes, and it is not suspended from any of the veins, then you should incise it as one would incise nodes, and extract it with what contains the skin and bursa; and otherwise, you should scrutinize all its being, and accordingly cure the location where it needs to be cured until it is healed.
Chapter XLV.
On the Section of the Species of Nodes.
Of the nodes, many species and you have already said all their species in the division.
And it is necessary that you recount thus the difference between a node and an excrescence, so as to dispel doubt.
Therefore, you say that with an excrescence there is heat and fever and violent pains until the ebullition of the superfluity is calmed; and it is completed putrefaction which then ceases the fever, the pain, and the heat.
But with a node there is no heat or fever or pain, and it contains that ciphac lion which is in its own sack.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a cheese and is made thus as a vessel, whether major or minor, and they are two species, either fatty and containing moisture, and the colors of the moisture are many, according to what you said in the division.
Therefore, when you come to the cure of a node, that you expose it and inquire into its beginning with a instrument which is called an intrumentum, according to what will come forth in the chapter which is appropriate to this.
And it is the manner of inquiring into abscesses and all nodes, that you take this instrument and introduce it into the most humid location which you find in the abscess, and you turn the points of the instrument little by little until you know that the instrument has penetrated to the cord.
And accordingly, your hand should be last according to the measure of the greatness of the abscess, and accordingly extract the intrumentum and consider what is situated in its vestige.
And if the moisture current is forgotten, of whatever color it may be, then you should incise it with a superficial section according to what I have said in other abscesses.
And if from the vestige of the intrumentum there is no moisture, then you should know that it is fat.
And dry it over with a section having the form of a cross according to this form: Thus as I have taught you, and suspend it with one incision, and excise the cord from all parts with ease, and preserve the skin or sack, if possible, over that and extract that pus from the node.
But if it is broken in the operation, then it is not possible to extract that pus, and many times it happens so, and accordingly you should extract it piecemeal until there is no longer any cause from it.
For if from it remains no cause, a little or too much, the node will return to its former state.
And if you find that from it remains some small part, then complete the wound with certain corrosive powders acute and strengthen the wound, and pause over that which is seated apostema cautiously, and cure that from other cure until it is healed.
And if it is a great node, then you should close the two lips of it and cure it with that which nourishes.
But if the pulsating vein is opposite to it, or non-pulsating, then you should compress and apply pressure to the location of threefold stitches that are vertigo-like, and let that rest for two or three days until the wound is quieted and the ebullition of blood ceases; and accordingly return to incising what remains of the node.

Chapter XLVII.
On the Cure of Men's Breasts which are assembled like Women's.
Sometimes the breasts of some men become swollen as they begin to sleep, and accordingly they become swollen, and the breasts like those of women, apostematosed, distant.
And to that one who has this condition, it is necessary to incise those parts with a lunar section according to this form, from the line of .B. to the line of .G., and accordingly excise all the swelling, and accordingly fill the wound with an incarnate medicine, and accordingly adjust the two lips of the wound with stitching, and cure that one until it is healed.
But if the breast declines to its parts and is softened by its magnitude, just like in women, then it should be cut into two equal parts, with two sections resembling a lunar figure, of which each one should be contained within the other until the end of each one, so that the major line contains the minor according to this form from the line of .B. to the line of .G. And accordingly excise the cord which is between the two lips of the wound, and stop the swelling, and administer that which we have said about suturing and powders which are necessary for the cure until it is healed.
And if you do not complete the incision of that which should be cut because of the unease of the patient and the flow of blood, then it is necessary to fill the wound with a submergent potion in corrosive oil and leave that one until the corroded part that remains of the grayness, and accordingly cure that one until it is healed.
Chapter XLVIII.
On the Perforation of Abscesses that come forth with Lichenification.
Those abscesses that come forth with lichenification are of the species of scrofulae, hard ones, which contain a bag of skin.
And some of those contain humidity; and those that contain humidity must be incised with a lunar section according to this figure: from the line of .G. to the line of .B. And when all the pus is evacuated from the abscess, then you should fill it with good oil and leave it until the next day.
And accordingly, the oil should be stopped and the wound should be cured with a suitable poultice for that, until it is healed.
And if the abscess is of the species of nodes and scrofulae, then you should cut over it just as we have said in the section on scrofulae similarly.
And if the wound is extended in such a way that it becomes a poultice, then you should administer to it a cautery according to what we have said before.
Chapter XLIX.
On the Section of Abscesses that occur through Artery or Vein.
When an artery is broken and the cord which is above it becomes consolidated, then many times an abscess occurs. And the signs by which it is known that inflammation and abscess are due to the artery or vein: for when an abscess is caused by an artery, it is long aggregated in the precordium of the body; and when you impelate the abscess with those said ties, you will feel a stridor in it.
And that which is caused by a vein is rounded to the manifest of the chest.
And the section on this abscess is an error, and most especially in those that are situated near the armpits, groins, neck, and many other places of the body; and it is very great, therefore it is necessary to avoid its cure with iron, and that which is located in the extremities or at the head should be avoided.
But on that which is due to the inflammation of the orifice of the artery, you should cut in the cord section in length; and accordingly, you should make the section with scissors and accordingly apply it to the artery, and deliver from the tunics until it is uncovered.
And accordingly, you should sew that thread and make it penetrate into the other side, and tighten the artery with a double thread in two places,  which cause is to your eyesight in extracting two arteries which are in the temples.
And accordingly, you should push the place which is between two ligaments with a spatula until the blood that is in it flows out and the abscess is dissolved.
And accordingly, you should administer a cure in which fire is generated until the ligaments fall away; and accordingly, you should cure that one with suitable poultices for it until it is healed.
And if it is an abscess due to section of a vein, then you should hold the place with your hand on the abscess and the cord, and accordingly introduce the needle below the location which you have held with your hand, and make it penetrate; and be in that file double so that you can extract that one from the other side, and accordingly tie the abscess with a double ligament according to what I have said to you about the ligature of two parts with two extremities of the thread.
But if you fear that the thread will be extracted, then accordingly introduce a thread with that needle below all the abscess so that the incision of the first needle is made, and tighten the four threads in those places.
And accordingly, cut the abscess in the middle of it so that when what is in it is evacuated, you should incise the superfluity of the cord, and leave what remains tied.
And accordingly, apply to it the poultice which you have submerged in vinegar and oil, and accordingly administer the cure made with some and powders until it is healed.
Chapter L of the Abscess that occurs due to Torsion of Nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in the artery and vein, so too does an abscess occur in the nerves when it occurs through percussion or excessive travail, and similarly.
And it is made the most in the places of the assistants or the heel, or in any place where the joints are bad.
And it is a great abscess, and is akin to the color of another body, and is in the greater part of it disposed to its place, when it is stretched with great vehemence, and then in it the patient seems stupid.
And it is not an abscess aggregated in the precordium of the body, and therefore it remains outside, and it is bad on all sides and does not go to any parts before or behind.
Therefore, the one that is in the joints should not be sewn with iron, for very lightly it would bring about contraction.
But the one that is on the head or forehead should have the cord cut with a spatula. But if it is a small abscess, then you should take it away with forceps and cut it from the roots.
And if it is great, then you should suspend it with scissors, and excise that, and accordingly stop that; and adjust the wound with sutures, and cure that one until it is healed.
Chapter LI.
On the Section of Abscess which occurs in the Abdomen.
I have observed in some men many times that certain parts of their bellies and other parts of their bodies are called fungals by similarity to those with fungi, whose roots are subtle and the head is thick; and the lips of these are conversed, and it is of those that are small, and it is of those that are very magnificent.
And I have also seen one, and in the belly of it were two thorns which were more like fungi, and there was no difference between those white ones with fine roots.
And the lips of those were turned inward and split open, and moisture flowed from it all the time.
Therefore, you should incise them and cut into one in pieces that weigh 18 ounces. and the other weighs 6 ounces.

Chapter LII.
On the Cure of an UMBILICAL HERNIA.
An umbilical hernia is caused by many reasons; or by fissures of the cicatrix which is above the belly, and eyes of it bursting forth as if it were a bag or pouch according to what has been said in other ruptures; or by blood flowing from a vein or artery according to what has been said before, or by ventosity constricted in there.

And if it is due to blood, then accordingly, the abscess appears with these signs declining towards blackness.
But if it is due to nothing but flesh, then accordingly, the abscess is a great one, large and hard, and is situated according to a certain quantity.
But if it is due to ventosity, then accordingly, the touch of it is like an onion.
And the operation in that case is to consider at the time if the umbilical hernia is due to blood from an artery, or a vein, or ventosity; accordingly, you should be prohibited from curing it, for in that case there is fear and dread just as I have taught you in the chapter on abscesses caused by arteries and veins.

And accordingly, cut the middle of the abscess extended over the ligament and introduce the said thread into it and seek out the bag; and if you have not found nor guarded by the ligament, then accordingly, you should extract the remaining portion and push the bag towards the parts inside the belly.
And if you have reached the pouch, then accordingly, take it with scissors; and cut out the superfluities of it.
But if it is contrary to your artery or vein, then accordingly, circle around that much, and accordingly return to your operation; and take two needles and put them in those two strong threads and introduce two needles into the circuit which you have made around the abscess in a cross-like manner, so that they will penetrate; and accordingly, tighten the abscess in four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, stop the needles and leave the place until the flesh extended falls away by itself; or cut it when it is softened and falls apart, and accordingly cure that one with water as needed until it is healed.
And if the umbilical hernia is caused by flesh nothing in that place or due to humidity, then accordingly, you should conquer the abscess as we have said and extract the blood or humidity which you find in it, and accordingly cure that one with water as needed and consolidate the place.

On the Section on Hernia in the Abdomen.
Hernia in the abdomen is nothing but an accumulation of moisture in the white apron which is below the cord of the genitals containing the urine, and is called a ciphac.
And sometimes it is in the own apron which nature has prepared for the urine in the part of the bladder, until it is full and another urine; and it is the cord of the genitals between the white apron which we have said, and it is not that but a taut cord.
And the generation of this hernia is due to weakness which occurs in the genitals, for that reason that it flows out from them this matter, and sometimes it happens through a blow made on the genitals.
And this moisture has many colors, for the color of it either declines to citrine or sanguine, or red, or is blackish feculent, or white watery, and that is what is most.
And the signs by which it is known where this accumulation of water is so: for if it is in the white apron which we have said, then accordingly the apostema is rounded declining a little towards length, and appears like the figure of urine, and shows the genitals, for the moisture contains that from all parts.
And if the moisture is in the own apron, then accordingly the apostema is rounded with the part of urine and by that one estimates that it is another urine.
And if the moisture is between the cord of the genitals and the white apron, then accordingly it is cut off below the sentiment.
And when you want to know the color of the moisture, then accordingly you try out the color of the moisture in the apostema by inserting a stick from which the form is preceded; and according to that which is found in the vestige of the stick, judge what it is below.
Therefore, when we come to the cure of it with iron, then accordingly it is necessary that you command the patient to be flebotomized if this is possible for you, and sometimes fill up his body.
And accordingly lie on your back on some cause a little above, and pause below him many cloths.
And accordingly sit you on the left side of him, and command the minister to follow on the right side of him, which will extend her hand to one or two sides to the cord of the genitals and to the part of the navel.
And accordingly take a spatula late and cut the cord of the genitals in half through the middle location, to the length almost up to the pubic bone.
And be made the section according to equal measure distant from the line which should be drawn on the skin of the genitals, until it reaches the white apron inclusive.
And excise that, and observe, and be cautious for yourself that you do not insert it; and your excision with which that is excised by partition below, so that it is suspended in the urine more and subtly scrutinize the excision according to the quantity possible.
And accordingly pierce the white apron full of water with a wide perforation, and extract all the water.
And accordingly separate between two lips of the section with uncles, and stretch the white apron to the upper parts; and do not touch the cord of the genitals inclusive, and insert the white apron in that manner which is possible for you. And insert it with its totality or in pieces, and more so the subtle sides, for if you do not scrutinize the insertion of him carefully, there is no surety that the water will not return.
But if the urine proceeds from that outside of its cord in the hour of your operation, then when you have completed the section of the white apron, return to it, and accordingly adjust the two lips of the cord of the genitals with suturing, and accordingly cure it just like the other wounds, until it is healed.
And if you find the urine corrupted by another disease, then accordingly it is necessary that you loosen the vessels which are sore the suspender, and extract the urine, and accordingly cure it with what we have said.
But if the water is accumulated in two separate parts, then you know that there are two hernias; therefore, insert that part according to what you have done in the other equally; and if it seems to you that it is one operation, then you do it.
And accordingly you wash away the blood, and insert into the sections wool submerged in oil or rose oil; and pause before another wool which you have submerged in vinegar and oil, and spread it over two genitals and the navel; and pause above folded cloths and powders, and bind those above with a ligament according to this form:
and accordingly two cloths are pressed and filled with Carmenated wool according to this likeness; and the extremities are made with those which are premuted of cotton or wet wool, and added to the structure in the waistband of the breeches, and one of the extremities takes from the anus to the thigh, and the second takes from the part of the pubic bone, and two extractions above two cresses below and two other extractions above two garters, and all the structure is adjusted in the waistband of the breeches.
And sometimes this hernia is medicated artificially with a cauterizer in the place of section with iron; and it is that you take a cauterizer cutlery subtle and insert it into the cord of the genitals, and ignite according to what we have recounted, until the white apron containing the water is discovered, and then you take the cauterizer according to this form: and it is assembled in Greek letters.
And accordingly pierce through that white apron, and ignite until all the humidity is exhausted.
And accordingly stretch the white apron with uncles, and excise with uncles, and excise with a subtle cauterizer ignited; and insert that into what is possible for you until it radiates all of it; and be cautious not to let the fire touch the urine.
And you know that this operation is more saved and longer in terms of blood flow, and better than an operation with section.
But if it happens to you that an apostema is caught in the hour of section or at the hour of cauterization, or blood flow, then accordingly it is necessary that you cure which contrariety makes for you by that, with a treatment which is suitable for him according to what has been said.
But if the patient is poor and does not hope for a second operation due to its horror, then accordingly administer perforation according to what we have recommended here; and it is when you are certified by this signification that the hernia is watery.
Then accordingly you should make the patient lie on some elevated ceiling, and pierce the apostema in the lowest part of him with a spatula late, and pause the perforation according to the length of the body.
And be the aperture wide enough for all the water to flow out.
And accordingly pause on the place cotton and bind it and leave it, in order that the remaining water may flow out. And accordingly cure that until the wound is healed.
But if contrariety is against the flow of water in that hour you pierce it: and this is not done except for this reason because the white apron in the orifice of the wound makes contrariety and prohibits the exit of the water, then accordingly it is necessary to put into the wound a pain or instrument with which the water is extracted from the hydropisies, the form of which is preceded; or adjust a small one in the opposite part of the wound.
And you know that the water returns, and is accumulated accordingly for about 2 months or thereabouts; and when it is accumulated, then accordingly it should be pierced according to that manner of procedure, in order that the patient may thus pass his days.
Chapter LXIII.
On the Section about Fleshly Hernia and its Cure.
Know that in the section about this hernia there is fear of being lost to perdition in greater dispositions; therefore, it has been seen by me the dismissal of him and being saved through that which is better.
And you should remember the operation in this case and the species of the apostemes.
Therefore, I say that the apostemes are born in those whose genitals are composed and it is made so by many causes: or by acute and bitter superfluity, which is poured into the genitals, or by impact.
And it is the color of the apostema according to the color of the body, and it is not found in him pain, and it is a grave apostema.
And very often it is a fatty apostema, the color of which is dark, and there is no sentiment in him.
And sometimes it is caused by blockage of the arteries, and by inflammation of those so that already there is a recollection of him, or by inflammation of veins.
But that which is caused by inflammation of the arteries is known by the distribution of the apostema when you compress it with those said ties; and it does not need for him to touch.
But that which is caused by inflammation of the vein is not dispersed from him for any reason, even with compression using those said ties.
And it is possible that on this species a section should be made and on the other flesh; and it is that you insert the cord of the genitals, and accordingly stretch the urine to the upper parts, and extract that from the white apron, and deliver the suspender of the vessels, and bind them, and insert the suspender more than you deliver that of all part of the parts of the urine.
Therefore, if the urine is already consolidated with those fleshly matters, then accordingly it is necessary that you extract the urine and insert it.
And if there is adhesion due to some cause of the white apron or between what is between the vessels, then accordingly it is necessary that you deliver all that adhesion and insert it with a rounded insertion.
But if it is a natural growth of flesh in the place of adhesion which is behind, then accordingly it is necessary that you insert all that is of him, and extract the urine just as we have said.
Therefore, when your operation is complete, then accordingly fill the wound with wool infused in rose oil and vinegar; and accordingly cure that with another cure until it is healed.
Chapter LXIV.
On the Cure of Hernia which is of Vine.
Vine is an aposteme enveloped by some wrapping resembling a sprout with softening of the testicles, and it is made difficult for the patient to perform movements, exercise, and walking.
And in the cure of this disease it is due to fear, because of which memory precedes it.
However, I must say that the operation in this case should be done according to what I did first.
And I say that it is necessary to make the patient lie on an elevated ceiling; and accordingly, enfold the suspender of the testicles at the lower parts; and accordingly, take hold of the cord of the genitals with those said ties and vessels which are proved by the sheath; and hold these other ministers that you have, and extend them with a strong extension.
And accordingly insert into the spatula late with a cross section on the right side of the vessels, until the vessels are discovered.
And accordingly excise from all parts just as we have said in extraction of arteries which are on the temples.
And accordingly place in those an awl with a faithful double thread, and tie that to the first of the places where it occurs, and tie that other time at the last of them. And accordingly insert those in the middle location by straight section according to the length of the body, and extract that which is accumulated from the foul and corrupted humors.
And accordingly cure the wound with the cure of other wounds in which you wish to be generated by that which falls from the vessels which nourish one of two genitals, for it is not bad because of this.
But if the vit affects all the vessels, then accordingly it is necessary that the testicles be extracted or that the testicles by this means be joined with the vessels, in order to be deprived of all nourishment by the section of the vessels, and to be extinguished, or to become hardened because it will not be nourished by them.
Chapter LXV.
On the Cure of Hernia Intestinal.
The advent of this hernia is due to a section that takes place in the apron extended over the belly from the navel to the groin, so that bursting forth from one of those intestines.
And this rupture is either of the apron or of excess of him, and it occurs in two species due to many causes: or percussion, or by force, or by noise, or by elevation of some grave cause, or by similarities.
And the sign of this, when it is due to extension of the apron, is that it occurs gradually over a long period of time, and does not occur suddenly, and it is an aposteme which grows evenly within the prepuce because the apron compresses the intestine.
And the sign of him is when it is due to section of the apron, because at the beginning it causes sudden and violent pain; and it is an aposteme which appears differently underneath the apparent cord, and this is because of the intestine's protrusion, and his body goes out to the parts outside the apron.
And sometimes it is forgotten in the intestine's membrane, and this is called intestinal hernia cirrhotic.
And sometimes it is made with ventosity, and sometimes it runs into the intestine's fissure, and here it is retained, and this is accompanied by great pain for the patient, and sometimes it makes a wicked and greater pain when it is pressed.
And in the cure of this disease's species with iron there is fear, because it is necessary to avoid the mistake in that.
And the manner of the operation in that is that you command the patient to reduce the intestine with his own hand to the parts outside of his conqueror if he obeys his returnment, and accordingly it should be enveloped over his back between your hands, and raise the patient's legs.
And accordingly take hold of the cord which cuts off the cord of the testicles to the upper parts, and insert the cord of the testicles all along; and accordingly place in two lips of the section uncis according to what is needed to open the section with those.
And if the section is according to the quantity that it is possible for you to forget from that, the urine should be forgotten.
And accordingly excise those apron-like things which are underneath the cord of the testicles, until the white hard apron is discovered in each part.
Then accordingly you should tie the said thread to that which is tied to the testicle, in that which is between the white apron and the one that is underneath the cord of the urine, and between the second apron, and absolve from that adhesion which is in the part behind the urine.
And accordingly take hold of the cord of the testicles with your left hand, and extend the white apron on top with your right hand, and raise the urine to the part of the section; and command the minister to extend the urine upwards and you dissolve the adhesion that is behind with complete solution.
And search for those said threads so that there may be no cause of the intestine being enveloped in the white hard apron. But if you find any cause from him, then accordingly you should tie that to the belly below.
And accordingly insert into the part underneath the cord of the testicles by section of which blood and pus will flow as we have recommended in that which we have said.
And accordingly apply wool soaked in oil to the wound; and pause there; and apply the thread which we have recommended (and sometimes the white apron is cauterized, which we have said should be done at the incision of the wound to prevent blood flow, because many times those apostemes occur) and let the thread fall into place.
But if the mistake of him is delayed, then accordingly cold water should be poured, and pause over that until it falls according to what we have said.
And accordingly cure the wound with another cure of wounds until it is healed.
Chapter LXVI.
On Hernia Ventosa.
In this hernia, it has not been seen that anyone has been burnt by the cure with iron.
And the first ones said that it is done just as we have recommended in the hernia which is with wine; which is that you tie the vessels accordingly at the section on that side with felicitous ease; and accordingly cut in the middle location around the aposteme with water that brings about that, until the vessels fall.
And cure the wound according to what we have said until it is healed.
Chapter LXVII.
On the Disproportion that is made in the Navel.
Sometimes a rupture occurs in the navel just as we have said, and protrudes the location.
And there is no reason for the genitals to be affected by the intestines at the genitalia; and if there is, it is slight; and returns to its normal state in all hours.
But if the time is prolonged with this, there is an opening in the apron into which the intestine descends; and this occurs because of the extension of the apron which is in the navel just as we have said, which cause is that the apron is stretched, and accordingly it becomes softened and the location apostematises and protrudes.
And the cure for this is with a cauterizing tool just as the narrative of this has described.
And sometimes it is cured with iron according to this manner: and it is that the patient lies on his back between his thighs, and accordingly cuts the location of the rupture of the apostemes protruding by section along the width in a quantity of three fingers.
And accordingly strengthen the white apron that is underneath the cord, until it is discovered.
And accordingly take the reed and pause it over the protruding location of the apron, and press it against the belly's fold.
And accordingly close the two protruding locations on the tip of the reed of the apron, and adjust them with a suture one to the other.
And accordingly extract the tip of the reed, and do not insert it into the apron completely, nor extend the urine, nor any other cause just as you would want to know in the cure of intestinal hernia.
And accordingly cure that with the cure of wounds, and by inserting the threads, cleanse it and restore the wound until it is consolidated; for the apron has contracted and the protrusion of rupture has not been adjusted.
And cauterization is better in this location, since it is closer to health.
Chapter LXVIII.
On Softening of the Cord of the Testicles.
Many times the cord of the testicles in some men is softened because the flesh which is between that is softened, and it is fitting for him to take care.
Therefore it is necessary for the one who is taking care of him in his cure to make the patient lie on his back, and insert all the softened cord which is above the natural cord into it; and accordingly adjust two lips with a suture.
And if you want, cut off the excess of the softened cord with three or four cauterizations and a firm suture.
And accordingly insert between the sutures; and accordingly cure by curing of other wounds until it is healed and the threads fall.
Chapter LXIX.
On Castration.
Know that castration in our law is excommunicated, but by reason it is written to me that I remember of it in this book; and it is not necessary to remind of it except by two manners, of which one is because of what this is in the knowledge of the doctor when he is asked about it, knowing the cure for him who suffers from it; and the other manner is that many times we have needed castration of other animals for our uses through them, just as with horses, and bulls, and pigs, and similar to these animals.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two manners: either by emasculation, or by incision at the section.
But in that which is done by emasculation, the way of the operation is that we make the animal lie down in cold water until its genitalia are softened and relaxed, and hang. And accordingly take them with the help of your hand into where they are resolved and do not appear to be left behind by the touch.
But in castration done by incision and section, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the cord of the testicles with your left hand.
And accordingly tie the suspenders, and cut on each side with one incision until two incisions process.
And accordingly cut those more than you would have excoriated. And do not leave any cause on those of the apron, except for the subtle apron which is above the vessels.
And this manner of castration is better which is done by emasculation, because emasculation leaves no cause for the testicles so that the animal desires to copulate.
And accordingly cure the wound until it is healed.
Chapter LXX.
On The Cure of Hermaphrodism.
Hermaphrodism in men is according to two manners: the one is when it appears as a space in the cord of testicles, in that which is between two testicles, a figure like that of a woman's vulva.
And sometimes from it flows urine which is in the cord of the testicles.
But in women, there is a species: and it is that the vulva of a woman on her pubic mound is like two testicles of a man, almost all of them being placed outside just as the male genitalia are.
And the cure is of three species: two of men and one of women's. And it is that you must insert those adjusted flesh into where it escapes the impression of that, and accordingly cure those with the cure of other wounds.
And in the second species of men from which urine flows which is in the cord of the testicles, there is no cure nor operation in it.
Chapter XXI.
On Swelling and Flesh Elevation due to Causes of Women.
Swelling is slightly adjusted on natural causes until it becomes exhausted and makes the regulation firm; and sometimes it is magnified in some women to such an extent that it spreads like in men and penetrates into coitus.
Therefore it is necessary for you to take the excess of swelling with your hand or with a tool and insert it.
And do not stop in inserting, especially at the pubic region, so that there may not be a flow of blood.
And accordingly cure that with the cure of wounds until it is healed.
But born flesh is flesh that grows in the horrificity of the mare until it fills it, and slightly rises to the outer parts according to the shape of a hen (and for this reason some first call this "diseased swelling"); therefore it is necessary for us to insert as we insert swelling, and cure that until it is healed.
Chapter LXXII.
On The Cure of Ulceration.
Ulceration is when the vulva of a woman is not perforated, or if it is slightly torn. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to disease that has preceded, and it is caused either by flesh not being adjusted or by subtle apron or thick apron.
And it is caused either in the pubic region of the mare, or in its sides, or at the highest part of it, or at the lowest part of it, and prohibits coitus and engagement and childbearing, and very much prohibits menstruation.
And the knowledge of that is made by observation of the uterus, if the opacity is apparent, you can diagnose; but if it is not apparent, you must search for it with those said fingers or with a reed.
But if the opacity is due to subtle apron and is placed on both lips, then you must cut and tear it.
And it is that you pause over the two lips of dust, and accordingly pause those said indications on both sides.
And the woman should be on her back, her arms extended, and accordingly extend both lips with force until that subtle apron is broken and the opacity is opened.
And accordingly take linen and soak it in oil and pause that over the location, and make the patient lie down at home for a day so that the inserted location is not consolidated.
But if the apron is thick and dense, it is necessary for you to insert that with a spatula or with a mulberry leaf.
But if the opacity is due to edema, then suspend that with a tool, and insert it.
And be familiar with the medicines that stop bleeding without causing injury, such as aconite, dragon's blood, and essence, adjusted with white of egg.
And accordingly administer a leaden cannula that is wide enough so that the wound does not become slightly consolidated.
And keep that for some days and administer lukewarm water.
And accordingly be cured with the other cure of consolidation until it is healed.
And sometimes accidents happen in the natural birth of other flesh, and accordingly it is necessary to insert according to this manner. But if it is due to gangrenous aposteme, there should not be any opposition with iron in any manner at the aposteme which is in the mare.

Chapter LXXV.
To teach midwives how to medicate abnormal births when they occur according to a non-natural figure.
A necessary cause is that the midwife should know at the beginning the figure of normal childbirth by its signs, and thus when you see the woman expelling at the parting points below and desires to smell the air, and on top of that she is relaxed because what comes out of her is from the part, and hastens the emergence of the fetus, then you should know that this part is according to a natural figure, and it is the emergence of the baby over its head, and the umbilical cord with that or suspended or hanging from the navel.
